data-height=&quot;360&quot; data-bandwidth=&quot;710376&quot; data-framerate=&quot;25&quot;/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;"><img alt="File:Bits &amp; Pieces - BP374 - Test flight of Pescara&#39;s helicopter - 1922 - EYE FLM7760 - OB105716.ogv" style="width:220px;height:176px" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Bits_%26_Pieces_-_BP374_-_Test_flight_of_Pescara%27s_helicopter_-_1922_-_EYE_FLM7760_-_OB105716.ogv/220px--Bits_%26_Pieces_-_BP374_-_Test_flight_of_Pescara%27s_helicopter_-_1922_-_EYE_FLM7760_-_OB105716.ogv.jpg" /><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/95/Bits_%26_Pieces_-_BP374_-_Test_flight_of_Pescara%27s_helicopter_-_1922_-_EYE_FLM7760_-_OB105716.ogv" title="Play media" target="new"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bits_%26_Pieces_-_BP374_-_Test_flight_of_Pescara%27s_helicopter_-_1922_-_EYE_FLM7760_-_OB105716.ogv" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Silent film of a test flight of Pescara\'s helicopter, 1922. <a href="/wiki/EYE_Film_Institute_Netherlands" title="EYE Film Institute Netherlands">EYE Film Institute Netherlands</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>In the early 1920s, Argentine <a href="/wiki/Ra%C3%BAl_Pateras_Pescara" title="Ra\xc3\xbal Pateras Pescara">Ra\xc3\xbal Pateras-Pescara de Castelluccio</a>, while working in Europe, demonstrated one of the first successful applications of cyclic pitch.<sup id="cite_ref-Munson_6-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Munson-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Coaxial, contra-rotating, biplane rotors could be warped to cyclically increase and decrease the lift they produced. The rotor hub could also be tilted forward a few degrees, allowing the aircraft to move forward without a separate propeller to push or pull it. Pateras-Pescara was also able to demonstrate the principle of <a href="/wiki/Autorotation" title="Autorotation">autorotation</a>.  By January 1924, Pescara\'s helicopter No. 1 was tested but was found to be underpowered and could not lift its own weight. His 2F fared better and set a record.<sup id="cite_ref-PPdist_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PPdist-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> The British government funded further research by Pescara which resulted in helicopter No. 3, powered by a 250&#160;hp radial engine which could fly for up to ten minutes.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Oemichen2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Oemichen2.jpg/220px-Oemichen2.jpg" width="220" height="100" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Oemichen2.jpg/330px-Oemichen2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Oemichen2.jpg/440px-Oemichen2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="452" data-file-height="206" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Oemichen2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Oehmichen N\xc2\xb02, 1923</div></div></div>\n<p>On 14 April 1924 Frenchman <a href="/wiki/%C3%89tienne_Oehmichen" title="\xc3\x89tienne Oehmichen">\xc3\x89tienne Oehmichen</a> set the first helicopter world record recognized by the <i><a href="/wiki/F%C3%A9d%C3%A9ration_A%C3%A9ronautique_Internationale" title="F\xc3\xa9d\xc3\xa9ration A\xc3\xa9ronautique Internationale">F\xc3\xa9d\xc3\xa9ration A\xc3\xa9ronautique Internationale</a></i> (FAI), flying his <a href="/wiki/Quadcopter" title="Quadcopter">quadrotor helicopter</a> 360 meters (1,181&#160;ft).<sup id="cite_ref-EOdist1_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EOdist1-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> On 18 April 1924, Pescara beat Oemichen\'s record, flying for a distance of 736 meters<sup id="cite_ref-PPdist_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PPdist-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> (nearly a half mile) in 4 minutes and 11 seconds (about 8&#160;mph, 13&#160;km/h), maintaining a height of six feet (1.8 meters).<sup id="cite_ref-flight-2_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-flight-2-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> On 4 May, Oehmichen set the first 1&#160;km closed-circuit helicopter flight in 7 minutes 40 seconds with his No. 2 machine.<sup id="cite_ref-Munson_6-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Munson-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the US, <a href="/wiki/George_de_Bothezat" title="George de Bothezat">George de Bothezat</a> built the quadrotor helicopter <a href="/wiki/De_Bothezat_helicopter" title="De Bothezat helicopter">de Bothezat helicopter</a> for the United States Army Air Service but the Army cancelled the program in 1924, and the aircraft was scrapped.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Albert_Gillis_von_Baumhauer" title="Albert Gillis von Baumhauer">Albert Gillis von Baumhauer</a>, a Dutch aeronautical engineer, began studying rotorcraft design in 1923. His first prototype "flew" ("hopped" and hovered in reality) on 24 September 1925,<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> with Dutch Army-Air arm Captain Floris Albert van Heijst at the controls. The controls that van Heijst used were von Baumhauer\'s inventions, the <a href="/wiki/Helicopter_flight_controls" title="Helicopter flight controls">cyclic and collective</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Vodegel_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vodegel-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Voogt_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voogt-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Patents were granted to von Baumhauer for his cyclic and collective controls by the British ministry of aviation on 31 January 1927, under patent number 265,272.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>In 1927,<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Engelbert_Zaschka" title="Engelbert Zaschka">Engelbert Zaschka</a> from Germany built a helicopter, equipped with two rotors, in which a <a href="/wiki/Gyroscope" title="Gyroscope">gyroscope</a> was used to increase stability and serves as an energy accumulator for a <a href="/wiki/Gliding" title="Gliding">gliding</a> flight to make a landing. Zaschka\xe2\x80\x99s plane, the first helicopter, which ever worked so successfully in miniature, not only rises and descends vertically, but is able to remain stationary at any height.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1928, Hungarian aviation engineer <a href="/wiki/Oszk%C3%A1r_Asb%C3%B3th" class="mw-redirect" title="Oszk\xc3\xa1r Asb\xc3\xb3th">Oszk\xc3\xa1r Asb\xc3\xb3th</a> constructed a helicopter prototype that took off and landed at least 182 times, with a maximum single flight duration of 53 minutes.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1930, the Italian engineer <a href="/wiki/Corradino_D%27Ascanio" title="Corradino D&#39;Ascanio">Corradino D\'Ascanio</a> built his D\'AT3, a coaxial helicopter. His relatively large machine had two, two-bladed, counter-rotating rotors. Control was achieved by using auxiliary wings or servo-tabs on the trailing edges of the blades,<sup id="cite_ref-spenser_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-spenser-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> a concept that was later adopted by other helicopter designers, including Bleeker and Kaman. Three small propellers mounted to the airframe were used for additional pitch, roll, and yaw control. The D\'AT3 held modest FAI speed and altitude records for the time, including altitude (18&#160;m or 59&#160;ft), duration (8 minutes 45 seconds) and distance flown (1,078&#160;m or 3,540&#160;ft).<sup id="cite_ref-spenser_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-spenser-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CDAdist_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CDAdist-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the Soviet Union, Boris N. Yuriev and Alexei M. Cheremukhin, two aeronautical engineers working at the <i><a href="/wiki/TsAGI" title="TsAGI">Tsentralniy Aerogidrodinamicheskiy Institut</a></i> (TsAGI, the Central Aerohydrodynamic Institute), constructed and flew the TsAGI 1-EA single lift-rotor helicopter, which used an open tubing framework, a four-blade main lift rotor, and twin sets of 1.8-meter (6-foot) diameter, two-bladed anti-torque rotors: one set of two at the nose and one set of two at the tail. Powered by two M-2 powerplants, up-rated copies of the <a href="/wiki/Gnome_Monosoupape#Variants" title="Gnome Monosoupape">Gnome <i>Monosoupape</i> 9 Type B-2</a> 100 CV output <a href="/wiki/Rotary_engine" title="Rotary engine">rotary engine</a> of World War I, the TsAGI 1-EA made several low altitude flights.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> By 14 August 1932, Cheremukhin managed to get the 1-EA up to an unofficial altitude of 605 meters (1,985&#160;ft), shattering d\'Ascanio\'s earlier achievement. As the Soviet Union was not yet a member of the <a href="/wiki/F%C3%A9d%C3%A9ration_A%C3%A9ronautique_Internationale" title="F\xc3\xa9d\xc3\xa9ration A\xc3\xa9ronautique Internationale">FAI</a>, however, Cheremukhin\'s record remained unrecognized.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Nicolas_Florine" title="Nicolas Florine">Nicolas Florine</a>, a Russian engineer, built the first twin tandem rotor machine to perform a free flight. It flew in <a href="/wiki/Sint-Genesius-Rode" title="Sint-Genesius-Rode">Sint-Genesius-Rode</a>, at the <i>Laboratoire A\xc3\xa9rotechnique de Belgique</i> (now <a href="/wiki/Von_Karman_Institute" class="mw-redirect" title="Von Karman Institute">von Karman Institute</a>) in April 1933, and attained an altitude of six meters (20&#160;ft) and an endurance of eight minutes. Florine chose a co-rotating configuration because the gyroscopic stability of the rotors would not cancel. Therefore, the rotors had to be tilted slightly in opposite directions to counter torque. Using hingeless rotors and co-rotation also minimised the stress on the hull. At the time, it was one of the most stable helicopters in existence.<sup id="cite_ref-art-helicopter_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-art-helicopter-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Br\xc3\xa9guet-Dorand <i><a href="/wiki/Gyroplane_Laboratoire" class="mw-redirect" title="Gyroplane Laboratoire">Gyroplane Laboratoire</a></i> was built in 1933. It was a coaxial helicopter, contra-rotating. After many ground tests and an accident, it first took flight on 26 June 1935. Within a short time, the aircraft was setting records with pilot Maurice Claisse at the controls. On 14 December 1935, he set a record for closed-circuit flight with a 500-meter (1,600&#160;ft) diameter.<sup id="cite_ref-GLdist_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GLdist-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> The next year, on 26 September 1936, Claisse set a height record of 158 meters (520&#160;ft).<sup id="cite_ref-GLalt_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GLalt-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> And, finally, on 24 November 1936, he set a flight duration record of one hour, two minutes and 50 seconds<sup id="cite_ref-GLdur_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GLdur-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> over a 44 kilometer (27&#160;mi) closed circuit at 44.7 kilometers per hour (27.8&#160;mph). The aircraft was destroyed in 1943 by an <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">Allied</a> <a href="/wiki/Airstrike" title="Airstrike">airstrike</a> at <a href="/wiki/Villacoublay" class="mw-redirect" title="Villacoublay">Villacoublay</a> airport.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Arthur_M._Young" title="Arthur M. Young">Arthur M. Young</a>, American inventor, started work on model helicopters in 1928 using converted electric hover motors to drive the rotor head. Young invented the <a href="/wiki/Stabilizer_bar_(helicopter)" class="mw-redirect" title="Stabilizer bar (helicopter)">stabilizer bar</a> and patented it shortly after. A mutual friend introduced Young to Lawrence Dale, who once seeing his work asked him to join the Bell Aircraft company.  When Young arrived at Bell in 1941, he signed his patent over and began work on the helicopter. His budget was US$250,000 to build 2 working helicopters.  In just 6 months they completed the first Bell Model 1, which spawned the <a href="/wiki/Bell_Model_30" class="mw-redirect" title="Bell Model 30">Bell Model 30</a>, later succeeded by the Bell 47.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Autogyro">Autogyro</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Autogyro" title="Autogyro">Autogyro</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pitcairn_Autogiro_NASA_GPN-2000-001990.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Pitcairn_Autogiro_NASA_GPN-2000-001990.jpg/220px-Pitcairn_Autogiro_NASA_GPN-2000-001990.jpg" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Pitcairn_Autogiro_NASA_GPN-2000-001990.jpg/330px-Pitcairn_Autogiro_NASA_GPN-2000-001990.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Pitcairn_Autogiro_NASA_GPN-2000-001990.jpg/440px-Pitcairn_Autogiro_NASA_GPN-2000-001990.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2033" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pitcairn_Autogiro_NASA_GPN-2000-001990.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Pitcairn_PCA-2" title="Pitcairn PCA-2">Pitcairn PCA-2</a> autogyro, built in the U.S. under licence to the Cierva Autogiro Company</div></div></div>\n<p>Early rotor winged flight suffered failures primarily associated with the unbalanced rolling movement generated when attempting take-off, due to <a href="/wiki/Dissymmetry_of_lift" title="Dissymmetry of lift">dissymmetry of lift</a> between the advancing and retreating blades. This major difficulty was resolved by <a href="/wiki/Juan_de_la_Cierva" class="mw-redirect" title="Juan de la Cierva">Juan de la Cierva</a>\'s introduction of the <a href="/wiki/Helicopter_rotor" title="Helicopter rotor">flapping hinge</a>. In 1923, de la Cierva\'s first successful <a href="/wiki/Autogyro" title="Autogyro">autogyro</a> was flown in Spain by Lt. Gomez Spencer. In 1925 he brought his <a href="/wiki/Cierva_C.6" title="Cierva C.6">C.6</a> to Britain and demonstrated it to the <a href="/wiki/Air_Ministry" title="Air Ministry">Air Ministry</a> at <a href="/wiki/Farnborough,_Hampshire" title="Farnborough, Hampshire">Farnborough, Hampshire</a>. This machine had a four blade rotor with flapping hinges but relied upon conventional airplane controls for pitch, roll and yaw. It was based upon an <a href="/wiki/Avro_504K" class="mw-redirect" title="Avro 504K">Avro 504K</a> fuselage, initial rotation of the rotor was achieved by the rapid uncoiling of a rope passed around stops on the undersides of the blades.\n</p><p>A major problem with the autogyro was driving the rotor before takeoff. Several methods were attempted in addition to the coiled rope system, which could take the rotor speed to 50% of that required, at which point movement along the ground to reach flying speed was necessary, while tilting the rotor to establish autorotation. Another approach was to tilt the tail stabiliser to deflect engine slipstream up through the rotor. The most acceptable solution was finally achieved with the <a href="/wiki/Cierva_C.19" title="Cierva C.19">C.19 Mk.4</a>, which was produced in some quantities; a direct drive from the engine to the rotor was fitted, through which the rotor could be accelerated up to speed. The rotor <a href="/wiki/Clutch" title="Clutch">clutch</a> was then disengaged before the takeoff run.\n</p><p>As de la Cierva\'s autogyros achieved success and acceptance, others began to follow and with them came further innovation. Most important was the development of direct rotor control through cyclic pitch variation, achieved initially by tilting the rotor hub and subsequently by the Austrian engineer <a href="/wiki/Raoul_Hafner" title="Raoul Hafner">Raoul Hafner</a>, by the application of a spider mechanism that acted directly on each rotor blade. The first production direct control autogyro was the <a href="/wiki/Cierva_C.30" title="Cierva C.30">C.30</a>, produced in quantity by Avro, <a href="/wiki/Liore_et_Olivier" class="mw-redirect" title="Liore et Olivier">Liore et Olivier</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Focke-Wulf" title="Focke-Wulf">Focke-Wulf</a>.\n</p><p>The production model, called the C.30A by <a href="/wiki/Avro" title="Avro">Avro</a>, was built under licence in Britain, France and Germany and was similar to the C.30P. It carried small movable trimming surfaces. Each licensee used nationally built engines and used slightly different names. In all, 143 production C.30s were built, making it by far the most numerous pre-war autogyro.\n</p><p>Between 1933 and 1936, de la Cierva used one C.30A (<i>G-ACWF</i>) to perfect his last contribution to autogyro development before his death in late 1936.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> To enable the aircraft to take off without forward ground travel, he produced the "Autodynamic" rotor head, which allowed the rotor to be spun up by the engine in the usual way but to higher than take-off r.p.m at zero rotor incidence and then to reach operational positive pitch suddenly enough to jump some 20&#160;ft (6 m) upwards.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Birth_of_an_industry">Birth of an industry</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:R-4_AC_HNS1_3_300.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/R-4_AC_HNS1_3_300.jpg/220px-R-4_AC_HNS1_3_300.jpg" width="220" height="175" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/R-4_AC_HNS1_3_300.jpg/330px-R-4_AC_HNS1_3_300.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/R-4_AC_HNS1_3_300.jpg/440px-R-4_AC_HNS1_3_300.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2107" data-file-height="1672" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:R-4_AC_HNS1_3_300.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Igor Sikorsky and the world\'s first mass-produced helicopter, the <a href="/wiki/Sikorsky_R-4" title="Sikorsky R-4">Sikorsky R-4</a>, 1944</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Helicopter_air_mail,_1947_.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/24/Helicopter_air_mail%2C_1947_.jpg/220px-Helicopter_air_mail%2C_1947_.jpg" width="220" height="153" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/24/Helicopter_air_mail%2C_1947_.jpg/330px-Helicopter_air_mail%2C_1947_.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/24/Helicopter_air_mail%2C_1947_.jpg/440px-Helicopter_air_mail%2C_1947_.jpg 2x" data-file-width="580" data-file-height="403" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Helicopter_air_mail,_1947_.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>First <a href="/wiki/Airmail" title="Airmail">airmail</a> service by helicopter in Los Angeles, 1947</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Focke" class="mw-redirect" title="Heinrich Focke">Heinrich Focke</a> at Focke-Wulf was licensed to produce the Cierva C.30 <a href="/wiki/Autogyro" title="Autogyro">autogyro</a> in 1933. Focke designed the world\'s first practical <a href="/wiki/Helicopter_rotor#Transverse" title="Helicopter rotor">transverse twin-rotor</a> helicopter, the <a href="/wiki/Focke-Wulf_Fw_61" title="Focke-Wulf Fw 61">Focke-Wulf Fw 61</a>, which first flew on 26 June 1936. The Fw 61 broke all of the helicopter world records in 1937, demonstrating a <a href="/wiki/Flight_envelope" title="Flight envelope">flight envelope</a> that had only previously been achieved by the autogyro.\n</p><p>During World War II, <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a> used helicopters in small numbers for observation, transport, and medical evacuation. The <a href="/wiki/Flettner_Fl_282" title="Flettner Fl 282">Flettner Fl 282 <i>Kolibri</i></a> <a href="/wiki/Synchropter" class="mw-redirect" title="Synchropter">synchropter</a>\xe2\x80\x94using the same basic configuration as <a href="/wiki/Anton_Flettner" title="Anton Flettner">Anton Flettner</a>\'s own pioneering <a href="/wiki/Flettner_Fl_265" title="Flettner Fl 265">Fl 265</a>\xe2\x80\x94was used in the Mediterranean, while the <a href="/wiki/Focke_Achgelis_Fa_223" class="mw-redirect" title="Focke Achgelis Fa 223">Focke Achgelis Fa 223 <i>Drache</i></a> twin-rotor helicopter was used in Europe.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>  Extensive bombing by the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">Allied forces</a> prevented Germany from producing any helicopters in large quantities during the war.\n</p><p>In the United States, Russian-born engineer <a href="/wiki/Igor_Sikorsky" title="Igor Sikorsky">Igor Sikorsky</a> and W. Lawrence LePage competed to produce the U.S. military\'s first helicopter. LePage received the <a href="/wiki/Patent" title="Patent">patent</a> rights to develop helicopters patterned after the Fw 61, and built the <a href="/wiki/Platt-Le_Page_XR-1" class="mw-redirect" title="Platt-Le Page XR-1">XR-1</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Francillon_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Francillon-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Meanwhile, Sikorsky settled on a simpler, single rotor design, the <a href="/wiki/VS-300" class="mw-redirect" title="VS-300">VS-300</a>, which turned out to be the first practical single lifting-rotor helicopter design.  After experimenting with configurations to counteract the torque produced by the single main rotor, Sikorsky settled on a single, smaller rotor mounted on the tail boom.\n</p><p>Developed from the VS-300, Sikorsky\'s <a href="/wiki/Sikorsky_R-4" title="Sikorsky R-4">R-4</a> was the first large-scale mass-produced helicopter, with a production order for 100 aircraft. The R-4 was the only Allied helicopter to serve in World War II, when it was used primarily for <a href="/wiki/Search_and_rescue" title="Search and rescue">search and rescue</a> (by the <a href="/wiki/USAAF" class="mw-redirect" title="USAAF">USAAF</a> <a href="/wiki/1st_Special_Operations_Wing#1st_Air_Commando_Group" title="1st Special Operations Wing">1st Air Commando Group</a>) in <a href="/wiki/Burma" class="mw-redirect" title="Burma">Burma</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> in Alaska; and in other areas with harsh terrain. Total production reached 131 helicopters before the R-4 was replaced by other Sikorsky helicopters such as the <a href="/wiki/Sikorsky_H-5" title="Sikorsky H-5">R-5</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sikorsky_R-6" title="Sikorsky R-6">R-6</a>. In all, Sikorsky produced over 400 helicopters before the end of World War II.<sup id="cite_ref-Day_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Day-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>While LePage and Sikorsky built their helicopters for the military, <a href="/wiki/Bell_Aircraft" title="Bell Aircraft">Bell Aircraft</a> hired <a href="/wiki/Arthur_M._Young" title="Arthur M. Young">Arthur Young</a> to help build a helicopter using Young\'s two-blade <a href="/wiki/Teetering_rotor" class="mw-redirect" title="Teetering rotor">teetering rotor</a> design, which used a weighted <a href="/wiki/Stabilizer_bar_(helicopter)" class="mw-redirect" title="Stabilizer bar (helicopter)">stabilizer bar</a> placed at a 90\xc2\xb0 angle to the rotor blades. The subsequent <a href="/wiki/Bell_30" title="Bell 30">Model 30</a> helicopter showed the design\'s simplicity and ease of use. The Model 30 was developed into the <a href="/wiki/Bell_47" title="Bell 47">Bell 47</a>, which became the first helicopter certified for civilian use in the United States. Produced in several countries, the Bell 47 was the most popular helicopter model for nearly 30 years.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Turbine_age">Turbine age</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Gas_turbine" title="Gas turbine">Gas turbine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Turboshaft" title="Turboshaft">turboshaft</a></div>\n<p>In 1951, at the urging of his contacts at the Department of the Navy, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Kaman" title="Charles Kaman">Charles Kaman</a> modified his <a href="/wiki/Kaman_K-225" title="Kaman K-225">K-225</a> <a href="/wiki/Synchropter" class="mw-redirect" title="Synchropter">synchropter</a> \xe2\x80\x94 a design for a twin-rotor helicopter concept first pioneered by <a href="/wiki/Anton_Flettner" title="Anton Flettner">Anton Flettner</a> in 1939, with the aforementioned <a href="/wiki/Flettner_Fl_265" title="Flettner Fl 265">Fl 265</a> piston-engined design in Germany \xe2\x80\x94 with a new kind of engine, the <a href="/wiki/Turboshaft" title="Turboshaft">turboshaft</a> engine. This adaptation of the <a href="/wiki/Gas_turbine" title="Gas turbine">turbine engine</a> provided a large amount of power to Kaman\'s helicopter with a lower weight penalty than piston engines, with their heavy engine blocks and auxiliary components. On 11 December 1951, the <a href="/wiki/Kaman_Aircraft" title="Kaman Aircraft">Kaman</a> K-225 became the first turbine-powered helicopter in the world. Two years later, on 26 March 1954, a modified Navy HTK-1, another Kaman helicopter, became the first twin-turbine helicopter to fly.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> However, it was the <a href="/wiki/Sud_Aviation" title="Sud Aviation">Sud Aviation</a> <a href="/wiki/A%C3%A9rospatiale_Alouette_II" title="A\xc3\xa9rospatiale Alouette II">Alouette II</a> that would become the first helicopter to be produced with a turbine-engine.<sup id="cite_ref-Connor-1_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Connor-1-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Reliable helicopters capable of stable hover flight were developed decades after fixed-wing aircraft. This is largely due to higher engine power density requirements than fixed-wing aircraft. Improvements in fuels and engines during the first half of the 20th century were a critical factor in helicopter development. The availability of lightweight <a href="/wiki/Turboshaft" title="Turboshaft">turboshaft</a> engines in the second half of the 20th century led to the development of larger, faster, and higher-performance helicopters. While smaller and less expensive helicopters still use piston engines, turboshaft engines are the preferred powerplant for helicopters today.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses">Uses</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HH-65C_Dolphin.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/HH-65C_Dolphin.jpg/150px-HH-65C_Dolphin.jpg" width="150" height="100" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/HH-65C_Dolphin.jpg/225px-HH-65C_Dolphin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/HH-65C_Dolphin.jpg/300px-HH-65C_Dolphin.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3008" data-file-height="2000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HH-65C_Dolphin.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An <a href="/wiki/HH-65_Dolphin" class="mw-redirect" title="HH-65 Dolphin">HH-65 Dolphin</a> demonstrating hoist rescue capability</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:112px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eesti_piirivalve_Mi-8.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Eesti_piirivalve_Mi-8.jpg/110px-Eesti_piirivalve_Mi-8.jpg" width="110" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Eesti_piirivalve_Mi-8.jpg/165px-Eesti_piirivalve_Mi-8.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Eesti_piirivalve_Mi-8.jpg/220px-Eesti_piirivalve_Mi-8.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1401" data-file-height="2100" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eesti_piirivalve_Mi-8.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Search_and_rescue" title="Search and rescue">Search and rescue</a> training in <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Mil_Mi-8" title="Mil Mi-8">Mil Mi-8</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Due to the operating characteristics of the helicopter\xe2\x80\x94its ability to take off and land vertically, and to hover for extended periods of time, as well as the aircraft\'s handling properties under low <a href="/wiki/Airspeed" title="Airspeed">airspeed</a> conditions\xe2\x80\x94it has been chosen to conduct tasks that were previously not possible with other aircraft, or were time- or work-intensive to accomplish on the ground. Today, helicopter uses include <a href="/wiki/Transport" title="Transport">transportation</a> of people and cargo, military uses, construction, firefighting, <a href="/wiki/Search_and_rescue" title="Search and rescue">Search and rescue</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tourism" title="Tourism">Tourism</a>, medical transport, law enforcement, agriculture, <a href="/wiki/Electronic_news_gathering" class="mw-redirect" title="Electronic news gathering">news</a> and <a href="/wiki/Motion_picture_photography" class="mw-redirect" title="Motion picture photography">media</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Aerial_photography" title="Aerial photography">aerial observation</a>, among others.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\nThey can be used for <a href="/wiki/Reflection_seismology" title="Reflection seismology">Reflection seismology</a> or <a href="/wiki/Recreation" title="Recreation">recreation</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Zepper-BK_117-C2-(EC145)-SchweizerischeRettungsflugwacht.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Zepper-BK_117-C2-%28EC145%29-SchweizerischeRettungsflugwacht.jpg/150px-Zepper-BK_117-C2-%28EC145%29-SchweizerischeRettungsflugwacht.jpg" width="150" height="100" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Zepper-BK_117-C2-%28EC145%29-SchweizerischeRettungsflugwacht.jpg/225px-Zepper-BK_117-C2-%28EC145%29-SchweizerischeRettungsflugwacht.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Zepper-BK_117-C2-%28EC145%29-SchweizerischeRettungsflugwacht.jpg/300px-Zepper-BK_117-C2-%28EC145%29-SchweizerischeRettungsflugwacht.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3742" data-file-height="2503" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Zepper-BK_117-C2-(EC145)-SchweizerischeRettungsflugwacht.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Eurocopter_EC145" title="Eurocopter EC145">Eurocopter EC145</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Swiss_Air-Rescue" class="mw-redirect" title="Swiss Air-Rescue">Swiss Air-Rescue</a> (REGA)</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sikorsky_Skycrane_carrying_house_bw.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Sikorsky_Skycrane_carrying_house_bw.jpg/150px-Sikorsky_Skycrane_carrying_house_bw.jpg" width="150" height="112" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Sikorsky_Skycrane_carrying_house_bw.jpg/225px-Sikorsky_Skycrane_carrying_house_bw.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Sikorsky_Skycrane_carrying_house_bw.jpg/300px-Sikorsky_Skycrane_carrying_house_bw.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="599" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sikorsky_Skycrane_carrying_house_bw.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Sikorsky_S-64" class="mw-redirect" title="Sikorsky S-64">Sikorsky S-64</a> Skycrane lifting a prefab house</div></div></div>\n<p>A helicopter used to carry loads connected to long cables or slings is called an <a href="/wiki/Aerial_crane" title="Aerial crane">aerial crane</a>. Aerial cranes are used to place heavy equipment, like radio transmission towers and large air conditioning units, on the tops of tall buildings, or when an item must be raised up in a remote area, such as a radio tower raised on the top of a hill or mountain. Helicopters are used as aerial cranes in the logging industry to lift trees out of terrain where vehicles cannot travel and where environmental concerns prohibit the building of roads.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> These operations are referred to as longline because of the long, single sling line used to carry the load.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The largest single non-combat helicopter operation in history was the disaster management operation following the <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_disaster" title="Chernobyl disaster">1986 Chernobyl nuclear disaster</a>. Hundreds of pilots were involved in <a href="/wiki/Airdrop" title="Airdrop">airdrop</a> and observation missions, making dozens of sorties a day for several months.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kfd-205-N408KC-050428-26cr.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Kfd-205-N408KC-050428-26cr.jpg/150px-Kfd-205-N408KC-050428-26cr.jpg" width="150" height="109" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Kfd-205-N408KC-050428-26cr.jpg/225px-Kfd-205-N408KC-050428-26cr.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Kfd-205-N408KC-050428-26cr.jpg/300px-Kfd-205-N408KC-050428-26cr.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2008" data-file-height="1464" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kfd-205-N408KC-050428-26cr.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Bell_204/205" title="Bell 204/205">Bell 205</a> dropping water onto a fire</div></div></div>\n<p>"<a href="/wiki/Helitack" title="Helitack">Helitack</a>" is the use of helicopters to combat <a href="/wiki/Wildland_fire_suppression" class="mw-redirect" title="Wildland fire suppression">wildland fires</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-usfs1_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usfs1-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> The helicopters are used for <a href="/wiki/Aerial_firefighting" title="Aerial firefighting">aerial firefighting</a> (water bombing) and may be fitted with tanks or carry <a href="/wiki/Helicopter_bucket" title="Helicopter bucket">helibuckets</a>. Helibuckets, such as the Bambi bucket, are usually filled by submerging the bucket into lakes, rivers, reservoirs, or portable tanks. Tanks fitted onto helicopters are filled from a hose while the helicopter is on the ground or water is siphoned from lakes or reservoirs through a hanging snorkel as the helicopter hovers over the water source. Helitack helicopters are also used to deliver firefighters, who <a href="/wiki/Rappel" class="mw-redirect" title="Rappel">rappel</a> down to inaccessible areas, and to resupply firefighters. Common firefighting helicopters include variants of the <a href="/wiki/Bell_205" class="mw-redirect" title="Bell 205">Bell 205</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sikorsky_S-64" class="mw-redirect" title="Sikorsky S-64">Erickson S-64</a> Aircrane helitanker.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Traumahawk_Loading_2.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/bb/Traumahawk_Loading_2.JPG/150px-Traumahawk_Loading_2.JPG" width="150" height="100" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/bb/Traumahawk_Loading_2.JPG/225px-Traumahawk_Loading_2.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/bb/Traumahawk_Loading_2.JPG/300px-Traumahawk_Loading_2.JPG 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="427" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Traumahawk_Loading_2.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Sikorsky_S-76" title="Sikorsky S-76">Sikorsky S-76C+</a> air ambulance</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HO3S-1_Korean_War.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/HO3S-1_Korean_War.jpg/150px-HO3S-1_Korean_War.jpg" width="150" height="105" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/HO3S-1_Korean_War.jpg/225px-HO3S-1_Korean_War.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/HO3S-1_Korean_War.jpg/300px-HO3S-1_Korean_War.jpg 2x" data-file-width="943" data-file-height="660" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HO3S-1_Korean_War.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">United States Navy</a> <a href="/wiki/Sikorsky_H-5" title="Sikorsky H-5">Sikorsky HO3S-1</a> in action during the <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a> (1950-1953)</div></div></div>\n<p>Helicopters are used as <a href="/wiki/Air_ambulance" class="mw-redirect" title="Air ambulance">air ambulances</a> for <a href="/wiki/Emergency_medical_services" title="Emergency medical services">emergency medical assistance</a> in situations when an <a href="/wiki/Ambulance" title="Ambulance">ambulance</a> cannot easily or quickly reach the scene, or cannot transport the patient to a medical facility in time. Helicopters are also used when patients need to be transported between medical facilities and air transportation is the most practical method. An air ambulance helicopter is equipped to stabilize and provide limited medical treatment to a patient while in flight. The use of helicopters as air ambulances is often referred to as "<a href="/wiki/MEDEVAC" class="mw-redirect" title="MEDEVAC">MEDEVAC</a>", and patients are referred to as being "airlifted", or "medevaced". This use was pioneered in the <a href="/wiki/Korean_war" class="mw-redirect" title="Korean war">Korean war</a>, when time to reach a medical facility was reduced to three hours from the eight hours needed in <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, and further reduced to two hours by the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_war" class="mw-redirect" title="Vietnam war">Vietnam war</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Police departments and other law enforcement agencies <a href="/wiki/Police_aviation" title="Police aviation">use helicopters</a> to pursue suspects. Since helicopters can achieve a unique aerial view, they are often used in conjunction with police on the ground to report on suspects\' locations and movements. They are often mounted with lighting and <a href="/wiki/Thermographic_camera" title="Thermographic camera">heat-sensing</a> equipment for night pursuits.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Westland_apache_wah-64d_longbow_zj206_arp.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Westland_apache_wah-64d_longbow_zj206_arp.jpg/150px-Westland_apache_wah-64d_longbow_zj206_arp.jpg" width="150" height="95" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Westland_apache_wah-64d_longbow_zj206_arp.jpg/225px-Westland_apache_wah-64d_longbow_zj206_arp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Westland_apache_wah-64d_longbow_zj206_arp.jpg/300px-Westland_apache_wah-64d_longbow_zj206_arp.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2876" data-file-height="1825" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Westland_apache_wah-64d_longbow_zj206_arp.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An <a href="/wiki/AgustaWestland_Apache" title="AgustaWestland Apache">AgustaWestland Apache</a> attack helicopter</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ukrainian_Ka-27PS_on_USS_Taylor_(FFG_50),_2010-A.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Ukrainian_Ka-27PS_on_USS_Taylor_%28FFG_50%29%2C_2010-A.jpg/150px-Ukrainian_Ka-27PS_on_USS_Taylor_%28FFG_50%29%2C_2010-A.jpg" width="150" height="100" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Ukrainian_Ka-27PS_on_USS_Taylor_%28FFG_50%29%2C_2010-A.jpg/225px-Ukrainian_Ka-27PS_on_USS_Taylor_%28FFG_50%29%2C_2010-A.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Ukrainian_Ka-27PS_on_USS_Taylor_%28FFG_50%29%2C_2010-A.jpg/300px-Ukrainian_Ka-27PS_on_USS_Taylor_%28FFG_50%29%2C_2010-A.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3872" data-file-height="2592" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ukrainian_Ka-27PS_on_USS_Taylor_(FFG_50),_2010-A.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_Navy" title="Ukrainian Navy">Ukrainian</a> Naval Aviation <a href="/wiki/Ka-27" class="mw-redirect" title="Ka-27">Ka-27</a> preparing for take off from the <a href="/wiki/USS_Taylor_(FFG-50)" title="USS Taylor (FFG-50)">USS <i>Taylor</i></a></div></div></div>\n<p>Military forces use <a href="/wiki/Attack_helicopter" title="Attack helicopter">attack helicopters</a> to conduct aerial attacks on ground targets. Such helicopters are mounted with <a href="/wiki/Missile_launchers" class="mw-redirect" title="Missile launchers">missile launchers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Minigun" title="Minigun">miniguns</a>. <a href="/wiki/Military_helicopter" title="Military helicopter">Transport helicopters</a> are used to ferry troops and supplies where the lack of an <a href="/wiki/Airstrip" class="mw-redirect" title="Airstrip">airstrip</a> would make transport via fixed-wing aircraft impossible. The use of transport helicopters to deliver troops as an attack force on an objective is referred to as "<a href="/wiki/Air_assault" title="Air assault">air assault</a>". <a href="/wiki/Unmanned_aerial_vehicle" title="Unmanned aerial vehicle">Unmanned aerial systems</a> (UAS) helicopter systems of varying sizes are developed by companies for military <a href="/wiki/Reconnaissance" title="Reconnaissance">reconnaissance</a> and <a href="/wiki/Surveillance_aircraft" title="Surveillance aircraft">surveillance</a> duties. Naval forces also use helicopters equipped with <a href="/wiki/Variable_depth_sonar" class="mw-redirect" title="Variable depth sonar">dipping sonar</a> for <a href="/wiki/Anti-submarine_warfare" title="Anti-submarine warfare">anti-submarine warfare</a>, since they can operate from small ships.\n</p><p>Oil companies charter helicopters to move workers and parts quickly to remote drilling sites located at sea or in remote locations. The speed advantage over boats makes the high operating cost of helicopters cost-effective in ensuring that <a href="/wiki/Oil_platform" title="Oil platform">oil platforms</a> continue to operate. Various companies specialize in this type of operation.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> is developing the <a href="/wiki/Mars_Helicopter_Scout" class="mw-redirect" title="Mars Helicopter Scout">Mars Helicopter Scout</a>, a 1.8&#160;kg (4.0&#160;lb) helicopter to be launched to survey Mars (along with a rover) in 2020. Given that the Martian atmosphere is 100 times thinner than that of Earth\'s, its two blades will spin at close to 3,000 revolutions a minute, approximately 10 times faster than that of a terrestrial helicopter.<sup id="cite_ref-Martian_helicopter_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martian_helicopter-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Market">Market</span></h3>\n<p>In 2017, 926 civil helicopters were shipped for $3.68 Billion, led by <a href="/wiki/Airbus_Helicopters" title="Airbus Helicopters">Airbus Helicopters</a> with $1.87 Billion for 369 rotorcraft, <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_Helicopters" class="mw-redirect" title="Leonardo Helicopters">Leonardo Helicopters</a> with $806 Million for 102 (first three quarters only), <a href="/wiki/Bell_Helicopter" title="Bell Helicopter">Bell Helicopter</a> with $696 Million for 132, then <a href="/wiki/Robinson_Helicopter" class="mw-redirect" title="Robinson Helicopter">Robinson Helicopter</a> with $161 Million for 305.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By October 2018, the in-service and stored helicopter fleet of 38,570 with civil or government operators was led Robinson Helicopter with 24.7% followed by Airbus Helicopters with 24.4%, then Bell with 20.5 and Leonardo with 8.4%, <a href="/wiki/Russian_Helicopters" title="Russian Helicopters">Russian Helicopters</a> with 7.7%, <a href="/wiki/Sikorsky_Aircraft" title="Sikorsky Aircraft">Sikorsky Aircraft</a> with 7.2%, <a href="/wiki/MD_Helicopters" title="MD Helicopters">MD Helicopters</a> with 3.4% and other with 2.2%.\nThe most widespread model is the piston <a href="/wiki/Robinson_R44" title="Robinson R44">Robinson R44</a> with 5,600, then the H125/<a href="/wiki/AS350" class="mw-redirect" title="AS350">AS350</a> with 3,600 units, followed by the <a href="/wiki/Bell_206" title="Bell 206">Bell 206</a> with 3,400.\nMost were in North America with 34.3% then in Europe with 28.0% followed by Asia-Pacific with 18.6%, Latin America with 11.6%, Africa with 5.3% and Middle East with 1.7%.<sup id="cite_ref-Flight17oct2018_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flight17oct2018-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Design_features">Design features</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rotor_system">Rotor system</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Helicopter_rotor" title="Helicopter rotor">Helicopter rotor</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Navy-hh1n-158256-070327-16cr-10.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Navy-hh1n-158256-070327-16cr-10.jpg/220px-Navy-hh1n-158256-070327-16cr-10.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Navy-hh1n-158256-070327-16cr-10.jpg/330px-Navy-hh1n-158256-070327-16cr-10.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Navy-hh1n-158256-070327-16cr-10.jpg/440px-Navy-hh1n-158256-070327-16cr-10.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Navy-hh1n-158256-070327-16cr-10.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A teetering rotor system with a weighted <a href="/wiki/Helicopter_rotor#Flybar_(stabilizer_bar)" title="Helicopter rotor">flybar</a> device</div></div></div>\n<p>The rotor system, or more simply <i>rotor</i>, is the rotating part of a helicopter that generates <a href="/wiki/Lift_(force)" title="Lift (force)">lift</a>. A rotor system may be mounted horizontally, as main rotors are, providing lift vertically, or it may be mounted vertically, such as a tail rotor, to provide horizontal thrust to counteract torque from the main rotors. The rotor consists of a mast, hub and rotor blades.\n</p><p>The mast is a cylindrical metal shaft that extends upwards from the transmission. At the top of the mast is the attachment point for the rotor blades called the hub. The rotor blades are attached to the hub. Main rotor systems are classified according to how the rotor blades are attached and move relative to the hub. There are three basic types: hingeless, fully articulated, and teetering; although some modern rotor systems use a combination of these.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anti-torque">Anti-torque</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Md500n.g-smac.arp.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Md500n.g-smac.arp.jpg/220px-Md500n.g-smac.arp.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Md500n.g-smac.arp.jpg/330px-Md500n.g-smac.arp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Md500n.g-smac.arp.jpg/440px-Md500n.g-smac.arp.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2816" data-file-height="2112" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Md500n.g-smac.arp.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>MD Helicopters 520N NOTAR</div></div></div>\n<p>Most helicopters have a single main rotor, but torque created by its <a href="/wiki/Aerodynamic_drag" class="mw-redirect" title="Aerodynamic drag">aerodynamic drag</a> must be countered by an opposed torque.\nThe design that <a href="/wiki/Igor_Sikorsky" title="Igor Sikorsky">Igor Sikorsky</a> settled on for his <a href="/wiki/Vought-Sikorsky_300" class="mw-redirect" title="Vought-Sikorsky 300">VS-300</a> was a smaller tail rotor. The tail rotor pushes or pulls against the tail to counter the torque effect, and this has become the most common configuration for helicopter design, usually at the end of a <i>tail boom</i>.\n</p><p>Some helicopters use other anti-torque controls instead of the tail rotor, such as the <a href="/wiki/Ducted_fan" title="Ducted fan">ducted fan</a> (called <i><a href="/wiki/Fenestron" title="Fenestron">Fenestron</a></i> or <i>FANTAIL</i>) and <a href="/wiki/NOTAR" title="NOTAR">NOTAR</a>. NOTAR provides anti-torque similar to the way a wing develops lift through the use of the <a href="/wiki/Coand%C4%83_effect" title="Coand\xc4\x83 effect">Coand\xc4\x83 effect</a> on the tail boom.<sup id="cite_ref-Frawley_Civil_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Frawley_Civil-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CH-47_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/CH-47_2.jpg/220px-CH-47_2.jpg" width="220" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/CH-47_2.jpg/330px-CH-47_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/CH-47_2.jpg/440px-CH-47_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1268" data-file-height="1012" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CH-47_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Boeing_CH-47_Chinook" title="Boeing CH-47 Chinook">Boeing CH-47 Chinook</a> is the most common dual rotor helicopter deployed today</div></div></div>\n<p>The use of two or more horizontal rotors turning in opposite directions is another configuration used to counteract the effects of torque on the aircraft without relying on an anti-torque tail rotor. This allows the power normally required to drive the tail rotor to be applied to the main rotors, increasing the aircraft\'s lifting capacity.  There are several common configurations that use the counter-rotating effect to benefit the rotorcraft:\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Tandem_rotors" title="Tandem rotors">Tandem rotors</a> are two counter-rotating rotors with one mounted behind the other.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Coaxial_rotors" title="Coaxial rotors">Coaxial rotors</a> are two counter-rotating rotors mounted one above the other with the same axis.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Intermeshing_rotors" title="Intermeshing rotors">Intermeshing rotors</a> are two counter-rotating rotors mounted close to each other at a sufficient angle to let the rotors intermesh over the top of the aircraft without colliding.</li>\n<li>Transverse rotors are pair of counter-rotating rotors mounted at each end of the wings or outrigger structures. They are found on <a href="/wiki/Tiltrotor" title="Tiltrotor">tiltrotors</a> and some earlier helicopters that had used then and now.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Quadcopter" title="Quadcopter">Quadcopters</a> have four rotors often with parallel axes (sometimes rotating in the same direction with tilted axes) which are commonly used on model aircraft.</li></ul>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Tip_jet" title="Tip jet">Tip jet</a> designs let the rotor push itself through the air and avoid generating torque.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Engines">Engines</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:GTD-350_Mil_Mi-2_helicopter_engine.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/GTD-350_Mil_Mi-2_helicopter_engine.JPG/220px-GTD-350_Mil_Mi-2_helicopter_engine.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/GTD-350_Mil_Mi-2_helicopter_engine.JPG/330px-GTD-350_Mil_Mi-2_helicopter_engine.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/GTD-350_Mil_Mi-2_helicopter_engine.JPG/440px-GTD-350_Mil_Mi-2_helicopter_engine.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="2736" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:GTD-350_Mil_Mi-2_helicopter_engine.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The turbine engine for the Mi-2 helicopter.</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_engine" title="Aircraft engine">Aircraft engine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Turboshaft" title="Turboshaft">Turboshaft</a></div>\n<p>The number, size and type of engine(s) used on a helicopter determines the size, function and capability of that helicopter design. The earliest helicopter engines were simple mechanical devices, such as rubber bands or spindles, which relegated the size of helicopters to toys and small models. For a half century before the first airplane flight, steam engines were used to forward the development of the understanding of helicopter aerodynamics, but the limited power did not allow for manned flight. The introduction of the <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">internal combustion engine</a> at the end of the 19th century became the watershed for helicopter development as engines began to be developed and produced that were powerful enough to allow for helicopters able to lift humans.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Early helicopter designs utilized custom-built engines or <a href="/wiki/Rotary_engine" title="Rotary engine">rotary engines</a> designed for airplanes, but these were soon replaced by more powerful automobile engines and <a href="/wiki/Radial_engines" class="mw-redirect" title="Radial engines">radial engines</a>. The single, most-limiting factor of helicopter development during the first half of the 20th century was that the amount of power produced by an engine was not able to overcome the engine\'s weight in vertical flight. This was overcome in early successful helicopters by using the smallest engines available. When the compact, <a href="/wiki/Flat_engine" title="Flat engine">flat engine</a> was developed, the helicopter industry found a lighter-weight powerplant easily adapted to small helicopters, although radial engines continued to be used for larger helicopters.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Turbine engines revolutionized the aviation industry, and the turboshaft engine finally gave helicopters an engine with a large amount of power and a low weight penalty. Turboshafts are also more reliable than piston engines, especially when producing the sustained high levels of power required by a helicopter. The turboshaft engine was able to be scaled to the size of the helicopter being designed, so that all but the lightest of helicopter models are powered by turbine engines today.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Special jet engines developed to drive the rotor from the rotor tips are referred to as <a href="/wiki/Tip_jet" title="Tip jet">tip jets</a>. Tip jets powered by a remote compressor are referred to as cold tip jets, while those powered by combustion exhaust are referred to as hot tip jets. An example of a cold jet helicopter is the <a href="/wiki/Sud-Ouest_Djinn" title="Sud-Ouest Djinn">Sud-Ouest Djinn</a>, and an example of the hot tip jet helicopter is the <a href="/wiki/YH-32_Hornet" class="mw-redirect" title="YH-32 Hornet">YH-32 Hornet</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Some <a href="/wiki/Radio-controlled_helicopter" title="Radio-controlled helicopter">radio-controlled helicopters</a> and smaller, helicopter-type <a href="/wiki/Unmanned_aerial_vehicle" title="Unmanned aerial vehicle">unmanned aerial vehicles</a>, use <a href="/wiki/Electric_motor" title="Electric motor">electric motors</a>. Radio-controlled helicopters may also have <a href="/wiki/Piston_engine" class="mw-redirect" title="Piston engine">piston engines</a> that use fuels other than gasoline, such as <a href="/wiki/Nitromethane#Use_as_an_engine_fuel" title="Nitromethane">nitromethane</a>. Some turbine engines commonly used in helicopters can also use biodiesel instead of jet fuel.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>There are also <a href="/wiki/Human-powered_helicopter" title="Human-powered helicopter">human-powered helicopters</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flight_controls">Flight controls</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Helicopter_flight_controls" title="Helicopter flight controls">Helicopter flight controls</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Helicopter_controls_layout.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Helicopter_controls_layout.svg/220px-Helicopter_controls_layout.svg.png" width="220" height="178" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Helicopter_controls_layout.svg/330px-Helicopter_controls_layout.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Helicopter_controls_layout.svg/440px-Helicopter_controls_layout.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="829" data-file-height="670" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Helicopter_controls_layout.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Controls from a <a href="/wiki/Bell_206" title="Bell 206">Bell 206</a></div></div></div>\n<p>A helicopter has four flight control inputs.  These are the cyclic, the collective, the anti-torque pedals, and the throttle. The cyclic control is usually located between the pilot\'s legs and is commonly called the <i>cyclic stick</i> or just <i>cyclic</i>. On most helicopters, the cyclic is similar to a joystick. However, the <a href="/wiki/Robinson_R22" title="Robinson R22">Robinson R22</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robinson_R44" title="Robinson R44">Robinson R44</a> have a unique teetering bar cyclic control system and a few helicopters have a cyclic control that descends into the cockpit from overhead.\n</p><p>The control is called the cyclic because it changes the <a href="/wiki/Blade_pitch" title="Blade pitch">pitch</a> of the rotor blades cyclically. The result is to tilt the rotor disk in a particular direction, resulting in the helicopter moving in that direction. If the pilot pushes the cyclic forward, the rotor disk tilts forward, and the rotor produces a thrust in the forward direction. If the pilot pushes the cyclic to the side, the rotor disk tilts to that side and produces thrust in that direction, causing the helicopter to hover sideways.\n</p><p>The collective pitch control or <i>collective</i> is located on the left side of the pilot\'s seat with a settable friction control to prevent inadvertent movement. The collective changes the pitch angle of all the main rotor blades collectively (i.e. all at the same time) and independently of their position.  Therefore, if a collective input is made, all the blades change equally, and the result is the helicopter increasing or decreasing in altitude.\n</p><p>The anti-torque pedals are located in the same position as the <a href="/wiki/Rudder" title="Rudder">rudder</a> pedals in a fixed-wing aircraft, and serve a similar purpose, namely to control the direction in which the nose of the aircraft is pointed.  Application of the pedal in a given direction changes the pitch of the tail rotor blades, increasing or reducing the thrust produced by the tail rotor and causing the nose to <a href="/wiki/Flight_dynamics" title="Flight dynamics">yaw</a> in the direction of the applied pedal. The pedals mechanically change the pitch of the tail rotor altering the amount of thrust produced.\n</p><p>Helicopter rotors are designed to operate in a narrow range of <a href="/wiki/Revolutions_per_minute" title="Revolutions per minute">RPM</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-crouch_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-crouch-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hawkRpm_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hawkRpm-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-newman_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newman-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> The throttle controls the power produced by the engine, which is connected to the rotor by a fixed ratio transmission. The purpose of the throttle is to maintain enough engine power to keep the rotor RPM within allowable limits so that the rotor produces enough lift for flight. In single-engine helicopters, the throttle control is a motorcycle-style <a href="/wiki/Twist_grip" class="mw-redirect" title="Twist grip">twist grip</a> mounted on the collective control, while dual-engine helicopters have a power lever for each engine.\n</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Swashplate_(helicopter)" class="mw-redirect" title="Swashplate (helicopter)">swashplate</a> controls the collective and cyclic pitch of the main blades. The swashplate moves up and down, along the main shaft, to change the pitch of both blades. This causes the helicopter to push air downward or upward, depending on the <a href="/wiki/Angle_of_attack" title="Angle of attack">angle of attack</a>. The swashplate can also change its angle to move the blades angle forwards or backwards, or left and right, to make the helicopter move in those directions.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Flight">Flight</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div id="mwe_player_2" class="PopUpMediaTransform" style="width:220px;" videopayload="&lt;div class=&quot;mediaContainer&quot; data-height=&quot;360&quot; data-bandwidth=&quot;579680&quot; data-framerate=&quot;11.607160363116&quot;/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;"><img alt="File:Svalbard helicotper.ogv" style="width:220px;height:165px" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Svalbard_helicotper.ogv/220px--Svalbard_helicotper.ogv.jpg" /><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a4/Svalbard_helicotper.ogv" title="Play media" target="new"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Svalbard_helicotper.ogv" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Helicopter hovering over boat in rescue exercise</div></div></div>\n<p>There are three basic flight conditions for a helicopter: hover, forward <a href="/wiki/Flight" title="Flight">flight</a> and the transition between the two.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hover">Hover</span></h3>\n<p>Hovering is the most challenging part of flying a helicopter. This is because a helicopter generates its own gusty air while in a hover, which acts against the <a href="/wiki/Fuselage" title="Fuselage">fuselage</a> and flight control surfaces.  The end result is constant control inputs and corrections by the pilot to keep the helicopter where it is required to be.<sup id="cite_ref-lombar_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lombar-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Despite the complexity of the task, the control inputs in a hover are simple. The cyclic is used to eliminate drift in the horizontal plane, that is to control forward and back, right and left.  The collective is used to maintain altitude. The pedals are used to control nose direction or <a href="/wiki/Course_(navigation)" title="Course (navigation)">heading</a>. It is the interaction of these controls that makes hovering so difficult, since an adjustment in any one control requires an adjustment of the other two, creating a cycle of constant correction.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transition_from_hover_to_forward_flight">Transition from hover to forward flight</span></h3>\n<p>As a helicopter moves from hover to forward flight it enters a state called <a href="/wiki/Translational_lift" title="Translational lift">translational lift</a> which provides extra lift without increasing power.  This state, most typically, occurs when the airspeed reaches approximately 16\xe2\x80\x9324 knots, and may be necessary for a helicopter to obtain flight.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Forward_flight">Forward flight</span></h3>\n<p>In forward flight a helicopter\'s flight controls behave more like those of a fixed-wing aircraft.  Displacing the cyclic forward will cause the nose to pitch down, with a resultant increase in airspeed and loss of altitude. Aft cyclic will cause the nose to pitch up, slowing the helicopter and causing it to climb. Increasing collective (power) while maintaining a constant airspeed will induce a climb while decreasing collective will cause a descent. Coordinating these two inputs, down collective plus aft cyclic or up collective plus forward cyclic, will result in airspeed changes while maintaining a constant altitude. The pedals serve the same function in both a helicopter and a fixed-wing aircraft, to maintain balanced flight. This is done by applying a pedal input in whichever direction is necessary to center the ball in the <a href="/wiki/Turn_and_bank_indicator" class="mw-redirect" title="Turn and bank indicator">turn and bank indicator</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Safety">Safety</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a
