title="Royal International Air Tattoo">Royal International Air Tattoo</a>,  England</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Maximum_speed_limit">Maximum speed limit</span></h3>\n<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Helicopter&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span>  <small><i>(September 2008)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The main limitation of the helicopter is its low speed.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> There are several reasons a helicopter cannot fly as fast as a fixed-wing aircraft. When the helicopter is hovering, the outer tips of the rotor travel at a speed determined by the length of the blade and the rotational speed. In a moving helicopter, however, the speed of the blades relative to the air depends on the speed of the helicopter as well as on their rotational speed. The airspeed of the advancing rotor blade is much higher than that of the helicopter itself. It is possible for this blade to exceed the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_sound" title="Speed of sound">speed of sound</a>, and thus produce vastly increased <a href="/wiki/Wave_drag" title="Wave drag">drag</a> and vibration.\n</p><p>At the same time, the advancing blade creates more lift traveling forward, the retreating blade produces less lift. If the aircraft were to accelerate to the air speed that the blade tips are spinning, the retreating blade passes through air moving at the same speed of the blade and produces no lift at all, resulting in very high torque stresses on the central shaft that can tip down the retreating-blade side of the vehicle, and cause a loss of control. Dual counter-rotating blades prevent this situation due to having two advancing and two retreating blades with balanced forces.\n</p><p>Because the advancing blade has higher airspeed than the retreating blade and generates a <a href="/wiki/Dissymmetry_of_lift" title="Dissymmetry of lift">dissymmetry of lift</a>, rotor blades are designed to "flap"&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 lift and twist in such a way that the advancing blade flaps up and develops a smaller angle of attack. Conversely, the retreating blade flaps down, develops a higher angle of attack, and generates more lift. At high speeds, the force on the rotors is such that they "flap" excessively, and the retreating blade can reach too high an angle and stall. For this reason, the maximum safe forward airspeed of a helicopter is given a design rating called <a href="/wiki/VNE" class="mw-redirect" title="VNE">V<sub>NE</sub></a>, <i>velocity, never exceed</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> In addition, it is possible for the helicopter to fly at an airspeed where an excessive amount of the retreating blade stalls, which results in high vibration, pitch-up, and roll into the retreating blade.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Noise">Noise</span></h3>\n<p>During the closing years of the 20th century designers began working on <a href="/wiki/Helicopter_noise_reduction" title="Helicopter noise reduction">helicopter noise reduction</a>. Urban communities have often expressed great dislike of noisy aviation or noisy aircraft, and police and passenger helicopters can be unpopular making it some annoying because of the sound. The redesigns followed the closure of some city <a href="/wiki/Heliport" title="Heliport">heliports</a> and government action to constrain flight paths in <a href="/wiki/National_parks" class="mw-redirect" title="National parks">national parks</a> and other places of natural beauty.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vibration">Vibration</span></h3>\n<p>Helicopters also vibrate; an unadjusted helicopter can easily vibrate so much that it will shake itself apart. To reduce vibration, all helicopters have rotor adjustments for height and weight. Blade height is adjusted by changing the pitch of the blade. Weight is adjusted by adding or removing weights on the rotor head and/or at the blade end caps. Most also have vibration dampers for height and pitch. Some also use mechanical feedback systems to sense and counter vibration. Usually the feedback system uses a mass as a "stable reference" and a linkage from the mass operates a flap to adjust the rotor\'s <a href="/wiki/Angle_of_attack" title="Angle of attack">angle of attack</a> to counter the vibration. Adjustment is difficult in part because measurement of the vibration is hard, usually requiring sophisticated accelerometers mounted throughout the airframe and gearboxes. The most common blade vibration adjustment measurement system is to use a stroboscopic flash lamp, and observe painted markings or coloured reflectors on the underside of the rotor blades. The traditional low-tech system is to mount coloured chalk on the rotor tips, and see how they mark a linen sheet. Gearbox vibration most often requires a gearbox overhaul or replacement. Gearbox or drive train vibrations can be extremely harmful to a pilot. The most severe being pain, numbness, loss of tactile discrimination and dexterity.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Loss_of_tail-rotor_effectiveness">Loss of tail-rotor effectiveness</span></h3>\n<p>For a standard helicopter with a single main rotor, the tips of the main rotor blades produce a vortex ring in the air, which is a spiraling and circularly rotating airflow. As the craft moves forward, these vortices trail off behind the craft.\n</p><p>When hovering with a forward diagonal crosswind, or moving in a forward diagonal direction, the spinning vortices trailing off the main rotor blades will align with the rotation of the tail rotor and cause an instability in flight control.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>When the trailing vortices colliding with the tail rotor are rotating in the same direction, this causes a loss of thrust from the tail rotor. When the trailing vortices rotate in the opposite direction of the tail rotor, thrust is increased. Use of the foot pedals is required to adjust the tail rotor\'s angle of attack, to compensate for these instabilities.\n</p><p>These issues are due to the exposed tail rotor cutting through open air around rear of the vehicle. This issue disappears when the tail is instead ducted, using an internal impeller enclosed in the tail and a jet of high pressure air sideways out of the tail, as the main rotor vortices can not impact the operation of an internal impeller.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Critical_wind_azimuth">Critical wind azimuth</span></h3>\n<p>For a standard helicopter with a single main rotor, maintaining steady flight with a crosswind presents an additional flight control problem, where strong crosswinds from certain angles will increase or decrease lift from the main rotors. This effect is also triggered in a no-wind condition when moving the craft diagonally in various directions, depending on the direction of main rotor rotation.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>This can lead to a loss of control and a crash or hard landing when operating at low altitudes, due to the sudden unexpected loss of lift, and insufficient time and distance available to recover.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transmission">Transmission</span></h3>\n<p>Conventional rotary-wing aircraft use a set of complex mechanical gearboxes to convert the high rotation speed of gas turbines into the low speed required to drive main and tail rotors. Unlike powerplants, mechanical gearboxes cannot be duplicated (for redundancy) and have always been a major weak point in helicopter reliability. In-flight catastrophic gear failures often result in gearbox jamming and subsequent fatalities, whereas loss of lubrication can trigger onboard fire.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Another weakness of mechanical gearboxes is their transient power limitation, due to structural fatigue limits. Recent EASA studies point to engines and transmissions as prime cause of crashes just after pilot errors.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By contrast, electromagnetic transmissions do not use any parts in contact; hence lubrication can be drastically simplified, or eliminated. Their inherent redundancy offers good resilience to single point of failure. The absence of gears enables high power transient without impact on service life. The concept of electric propulsion applied to helicopter and electromagnetic drive was brought to reality by <a href="/wiki/Pascal_Chretien" title="Pascal Chretien">Pascal Chretien</a> who designed, built and flew world\'s first man-carrying, free-flying electric helicopter. The concept was taken from the conceptual <a href="/wiki/Computer-aided_design" title="Computer-aided design">computer-aided design</a> model on 10 September 2010 to the first testing at 30% power on 1 March 2011 - less than six months. The aircraft first flew on 12 August 2011. All development was conducted in Venelles, France.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hazards">Hazards</span></h3>\n<p>As with any moving vehicle, unsafe operation could result in loss of control, structural damage, or loss of life. The following is a list of some of the potential hazards for helicopters:\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Settling_with_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Settling with power">Settling with power</a> is when the aircraft has insufficient power to arrest its descent.  This hazard can develop into Vortex ring state if not corrected early.<sup id="cite_ref-dtic.mil_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dtic.mil-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Vortex_ring" title="Vortex ring">Vortex ring</a> state is a hazard induced by a combination of low airspeed, high power setting, and high descent rate.  Rotor-tip vortices circulate from the high pressure air below the rotor disk to low pressure air above the disk, so that the helicopter settles into its own descending airflow.<sup id="cite_ref-dtic.mil_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dtic.mil-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Adding more power increases the rate of air circulation and aggravates the situation.  It is sometimes confused with settling with power, but they are aerodynamically different.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Retreating_blade_stall" title="Retreating blade stall">Retreating blade stall</a> is experienced during high speed flight and is the most common limiting factor of a helicopter\'s forward speed.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Ground_resonance" title="Ground resonance">Ground resonance</a> is a self-reinforcing vibration that occurs when the lead/lag spacing of the blades of an <a href="/wiki/Helicopter_rotor#Fully_articulated" title="Helicopter rotor">articulated rotor system</a> becomes irregular.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Low-G_condition" class="mw-redirect" title="Low-G condition">Low-G condition</a> is an abrupt change from a positive G-force state to a negative G-force state that results in loss of lift (unloaded disc) and subsequent roll over. If aft cyclic is applied while the disc is unloaded, the main rotor could strike the tail causing catastrophic failure.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Dynamic_rollover" title="Dynamic rollover">Dynamic rollover</a> in which the helicopter pivots around one of the skids and \'pulls\' itself onto its side (almost like a fixed-wing aircraft <a href="/wiki/Ground_loop_(aviation)" title="Ground loop (aviation)">ground loop</a>).</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Powertrain" title="Powertrain">Powertrain</a> failures, especially those that occur within the shaded area of the <a href="/wiki/Height-velocity_diagram" class="mw-redirect" title="Height-velocity diagram">height-velocity diagram</a>.</li>\n<li>Tail rotor failures which occur from either a mechanical malfunction of the tail rotor control system or a loss of tail rotor thrust authority, called "loss of tail-rotor effectiveness" (LTE).</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Brownout_(aviation)" class="mw-redirect" title="Brownout (aviation)">Brownout</a> in dusty conditions or <a href="/wiki/Whiteout_(weather)" title="Whiteout (weather)">whiteout</a> in snowy conditions.</li>\n<li>Low rotor RPM, or "rotor droop", is when the engine cannot drive the blades at sufficient RPM to maintain flight.</li>\n<li>Rotor overspeed, which can over-stress the rotor hub pitch bearings (brinelling) and, if severe enough, cause blade separation from the aircraft.</li>\n<li>Wire and tree strikes due to low altitude operations and take-offs and landings in remote locations.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Controlled_flight_into_terrain" title="Controlled flight into terrain">Controlled flight into terrain</a> in which the aircraft is flown into the ground unintentionally due to a lack of situational awareness.</li>\n<li>Mast bumping in some helicopters<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" margin:auto; text-align:center;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th rowspan="2" style="background-color: #efefef;">&#160;\n</th>\n<th style="background-color: #e6e6ff"><a href="/wiki/Aerostat" title="Aerostat">Aerostat</a>\n</th>\n<th colspan="3" style="background-color: #e6e6ff"><a href="/wiki/Aircraft#Heavier_than_air_\xe2\x80\x93_aerodynes" title="Aircraft">Aerodyne</a>\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<th style="background-color: #e6e6ff">Lift: Lighter than air gas</th>\n<th style="background-color: #e6e6ff">Lift: Fixed wing</th>\n<th style="background-color: #e6e6ff">Lift: Unpowered rotor</th>\n<th style="background-color: #e6e6ff">Lift: Powered rotor\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<th style="text-align:left; background-color: #e6e6ff">Unpowered free flight\n</th>\n<td>(Free) <a href="/wiki/Balloon_(aeronautics)" title="Balloon (aeronautics)">balloon</a></td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Glider_(aircraft)" title="Glider (aircraft)">Glider</a></td>\n<td><a class="mw-selflink selflink">Helicopter</a>, etc. in <a href="/wiki/Autorotation" title="Autorotation">autorotation</a></td>\n<td>(None \xe2\x80\x93 see note 2)\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th style="text-align:left; background-color: #e6e6ff">Tethered (static or towed)\n</th>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Tethered_balloon" title="Tethered balloon">Tethered balloon</a></td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Kite" title="Kite">Kite</a></td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Rotor_kite" title="Rotor kite">Rotor kite</a></td>\n<td>(None \xe2\x80\x93 see note 2)\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th style="text-align:left; background-color: #e6e6ff">Powered\n</th>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Airship" title="Airship">Airship</a></td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Airplane" title="Airplane">Airplane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ornithopter" title="Ornithopter">ornithopter</a>, etc.</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Autogyro" title="Autogyro">Autogyro</a></td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Gyrodyne" title="Gyrodyne">Gyrodyne</a>, <a class="mw-selflink selflink">helicopter</a>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n</div></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="navbox-list navbox-even hlist" style="width:100%;padding:0px;text-align:left"><div style="padding:0em 0.25em">\n<ul><li>Note 1: <span class="wrap">A <a href="/wiki/Tiltwing" title="Tiltwing">tiltwing</a> or <a href="/wiki/Tiltrotor" title="Tiltrotor">tiltrotor</a> aircraft functions as an aeroplane during normal (horizontal) flight and as a helicopter during low-speed flight.</span></li></ul>\n<ul><li>Note 2: <span class="wrap">For full-size aircraft with powered rotors the rotor is normally tilted to achieve thrust (e.g. in a helicopter). Some toys (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Balloon_helicopter" title="Balloon helicopter">balloon helicopter</a>) do have a powered rotor with no means to tilt the rotor to produce horizontal thrust.</span></li></ul>\n<ul><li>Note 3: <span class="wrap"><a href="/wiki/Ground_effect_vehicle" class="mw-redirect" title="Ground effect vehicle">Ground effect vehicles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hovercraft" title="Hovercraft">hovercraft</a> are not included in the table, nor are <a href="/wiki/Experimental_aircraft" title="Experimental aircraft">experimental aircraft</a> with novel thrust / lift solutions (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Coleopter" title="Coleopter">coleopter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rolls-Royce_Thrust_Measuring_Rig" title="Rolls-Royce Thrust Measuring Rig">Flying Bedstead</a>, <a href="/wiki/Avro_Canada_VZ-9_Avrocar" title="Avro Canada VZ-9 Avrocar">Avrocar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Flettner_airplane" title="Flettner airplane">flettner airplane</a>) or balloon-wing hybrids (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Kytoon" title="Kytoon">kytoon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_airship" title="Hybrid airship">hybrid airship</a>).</span></li></ul>\n</div></td></tr></tbody></table></div>\n<div role="navigation" class="navbox"
